The 2-adic valuation of k is not simply v_2(t) + s because subtracting a may affect the valuation. For example, s=8, t=1, t * 2^s = 256. Subtract a=8 gives 248. 248 = 8 * 31, v_2=3. f(a) | (b^a - f(b)^{f(a)})
f(b) | (a^b - f(a)^{f(b)})

We can try to combine them to get a bound on f(a) in terms of a and b. This argument is rigorous, provided we prove the lemma about gcd(n^n, 2^n - 1) вүӨ 4n, and also we need to justify that if d divides 2^k - 1 and also divides n^n, then d вүӨ 4n. The reasoning that "d divides 2^m - 1" does not force d to include the primitive prime divisor. So we cannot conclude that q divides d. Therefore, the contradiction from Zsigmondy is not valid. ## Solution
We need to determine the smallest constant \(c\) such that \(f(n)\le c\,n\) for all bonza functions \(f:\mathbb N\to\mathbb N\) and all positive integers \(n\).

**Preliminaries.**  For a function \(f:\mathbb N\to\mathbb N\) to be bonza we require

\[
f(a)\mid\bigl(b^{\,a}-f(b)^{\,f(a)}\bigr)\qquad\forall\,a,b\in\mathbb N .
\]

In particular we may set \(b=1\) (then \(f(1)=1\) is forced) and \(b=a\) to obtain the following necessary conditions.

1. **\(f(1)=1\).**  Indeed, with \(a=1\) and \(b\) arbitrary we have \(f(1)\mid b-f(b)^{\,f(1)}\).  Choosing \(b=2\) gives \(f(1)\mid 2-f(2)^{\,f(1)}\).  Because the rightвҖ‘hand side is at most \(2\) in absolute value when \(f(2)\ge1\) and \(f(1)\ge1\), the only possibility is \(f(1)=1\).  (A more systematic derivation: from \(a=1,b=2\) we get \(f(1)\mid 2-f(2)^{f(1)}\).  Since \(f(2)\ge1\), \(f(2)^{f(1)}\ge1\), so the difference is at most \(1\) in absolute value; if \(f(1)>1\) then \(f(1)\) would have to divide a number smaller than itself, impossible.  Hence \(f(1)=1\).)

2. **\(f(a)\mid a^{\,a}\) for every \(a\).**  Taking \(b=a\) gives \(f(a)\mid a^{\,a}-f(a)^{\,f(a)}\).  Because \(f(a)^{\,f(a)}\) is a multiple of \(f(a)\), we deduce \(f(a)\mid a^{\,a}\).

Now let \(n\) be an arbitrary positive integer and set \(d=f(n)\).  We shall prove that \(d\le 4n\) for every bonza function \(f\).  Afterwards we exhibit a bonza function for which \(f(4)=16\) (hence \(f(4)=4\cdot4\)), showing that the constant \(4\) is attainable, i.e. the smallest such \(c\) is \(4\).

---

### Lemma 1.
For every \(n\ge 1\),

\[
\gcd\!\bigl(n^{\,n},\;2^{\,n}-1\bigr)\le 4n .
\]

*Proof.*  Let \(p\) be an odd prime dividing \(\gcd(n^{\,n},2^{\,n}-1)\).  Then \(p\mid n\) (because \(p\mid n^{\,n}\)) and also \(2^{\,n}\equiv 1\pmod p\).  Hence the order of \(2\) modulo \(p\) divides both \(n\) and \(p-1\) (by FermatвҖҷs little theorem).  In particular the order is at most \(\min(n,p-1)\).

We consider the prime power exponent of \(p\) in the gcd.  Write \(n^{\,n}=\prod p^{e_p n}\) where \(e_p=v_p(n)\).  The power of \(p\) in \(2^{\,n}-1\) is not directly known, but the exponent of \(p\) in the gcd cannot exceed the exponent of \(p\) in \(n^{\,n}\), which is \(e_p n\).  The crucial observation is that the product of all such prime powers (the gcd) is at most \(4n\).  This can be proved by induction on \(n\) or by using the following simple inequality.

For any odd prime \(p\) dividing \(\gcd(n^{\,n},2^{\,n}-1)\), we have \(p\mid n\) and also \(2^{\,n}\equiv 1\pmod p\).  The order of \(2\) modulo \(p\) is a divisor of \(n\) and also of \(p-1\).  Consequently \(p-1\ge \operatorname{ord}_p(2)\le n\).  This doesnвҖҷt yet bound \(p\) itself.  However we can examine the 2вҖ‘adic part separately.  The number \(2^{\,n}-1\) has a certain 2вҖ‘adic valuation; we will treat it in the next lemma.  But for now, note that the gcd is a divisor of \(n^{\,n}\) and also of \(2^{\,n}-1\).  Since \(n^{\,n}\) is a product of powers of primes that divide \(n\), the gcd cannot contain a prime \(p\) with exponent exceeding \(n\cdot v_p(n)\).  The bound \(4n\) is much smaller than \(n^{\,n}\) for large \(n\), so the gcd must be small.  A standard approach uses ZsigmondyвҖҷs theorem and the fact that if a prime \(p\) divides \(2^{\,n}-1\) and also divides \(n\), then \(p\) must be a divisor of \(n\) and the order of \(2\) modulo \(p\) divides \(n\).  The largest possible such prime is roughly \(n\), but the product over all such primes of their powers in \(n^{\,n}\) is huge; however the gcd with \(2^{\,n}-1\) is restricted by the fact that \(2^{\,n}-1\) is not divisible by high powers of small primes unless the exponent \(n\) is special.  The inequality \(\gcd(n^{\,n},2^{\,n}-1)\le 4n\) can be verified by a short induction: suppose it holds for all numbers less than \(n\).  Then consider the prime factorization of \(n\).  The only primes that can appear in the gcd are those that divide both \(n\) and \(2^{\,n}-1\).  For each such prime \(p\) we have \(2^{\,n}\equiv 1\pmod p\) and \(p\mid n\).  This implies \(n\ge p\).  Moreover, the exponent of \(p\) in \(n^{\,n}\) is \(n\cdot v_p(n)\).  The exponent of \(p\) in \(2^{\,n}-1\) is at most \(n\cdot v_p(n)\) (trivial).  The product over all \(p\) of the maximum possible exponent in the gcd is still huge.  But we can prove a stronger statement: for any \(n\), the largest divisor of \(n^{\,n}\) that also divides \(2^{\,n}-1\) is actually a divisor of \(4n\).  This can be seen by considering the 2вҖ‘adic part and the odd primes.

LetвҖҷs separate the odd part and the 2вҖ‘part.  The number \(2^{\,n}-1\) is odd, so any common divisor with \(n^{\,n}\) must be odd if \(n\) is odd, but if \(n\) is even then \(n^{\,n}\) contains powers of 2.  The 2вҖ‘adic valuation of \(2^{\,n}-1\) is \(v_2(2^{\,n}-1)=v_2(n)+2\) for even \(n\ge 2\)?  Actually we have the known formula: for any integer \(a\), \(v_2(2^{\,n}-1)=v_2(n)+2\) when \(n\) is even, and \(v_2(2^{\,n}-1)=1\) when \(n\) is odd.  More precisely, if \(n\) is odd, \(2^{\,n}-1\) is divisible by \(2\) but not by \(4\).  If \(n\) is even, write \(n=2^k\cdot m\) with \(m\) odd, then \(v_2(2^{\,n}-1)=k+2\).  This can be proven using LTE.  The odd part of \(2^{\,n}-1\) is not directly bounded, but the common divisor with \(n^{\,n}\) is small.

A direct proof of the lemma can be given as follows.  Let \(g=\gcd(n^{\,n},2^{\,n}-1)\).  For any odd prime \(p\) dividing \(g\), we have \(p\mid n\) and \(2^{\,n}\equiv1\pmod p\).  Hence the order of \(2\) modulo \(p\) divides both \(n\) and \(p-1\).  In particular, \(p\ge \operatorname{ord}_p(2)+1\).  Also, because \(p\mid n\), we have \(p\le n\).  The product of all such primes raised to the exponent they have in \(n^{\,n}\) (which is \(n\cdot v_p(n)\)) can be huge, but we are only interested in the gcd, which is a divisor of \(2^{\,n}-1\) as well.  The key is to note that \(2^{\,n}-1\) is a specific number; its prime factors are those for which the order divides \(n\).  The largest possible such prime is at most \(2^{\,n}-1\), but we need a linear bound.  We can prove the lemma by induction on \(n\) using the following identity: if \(n\) is even, then

\[
\gcd(n^{\,n},2^{\,n}-1)=\gcd\bigl((n/2)^{\,n},2^{\,n}-1\bigr)\cdot 2^{\,v_2(n)+2} \text{ or something like that.}
\]

But this is messy.  LetвҖҷs try a different approach: we can prove a stronger statement: For all \(n\ge 1\),

\[
\gcd(n^{\,n},2^{\,n}-1)\le 4n .
\]

We'll prove it by contradiction.  Suppose there exists \(n\) with \(g>4n\).  Then \(g\) is a divisor of \(n^{\,n}\) and of \(2^{\,n}-1\).  Since \(g\) divides \(n^{\,n}\), all its prime factors are among the primes dividing \(n\).  Let \(p\) be an odd prime dividing \(g\).  Then \(p\mid n\) and \(2^{\,n}\equiv1\pmod p\).  Hence the order of \(2\) modulo \(p\) divides \(n\).  Also, by FLT, it divides \(p-1\).  So \(p\ge \operatorname{ord}_p(2)+1\).  Now, because \(g\) is a divisor of \(2^{\,n}-1\), we have \(g\le 2^{\,n}-1\).  But we need a contradiction with \(g>4n\).  Perhaps we can use the fact that if \(p\) is an odd prime dividing both \(n\) and \(2^{\,n}-1\), then \(p\) must be a divisor of \(n\) and also \(p\equiv 1\pmod{?}\).  The only way to get \(g>4n\) is if \(n\) is composite with many small prime factors, but then \(2^{\,n}-1\) is not very large; for large \(n\), \(2^{\,n}-1\) is huge, so \(g\) could be huge.  However \(g\) must divide \(n^{\,n}\), which for large \(n\) is enormous, but \(2^{\,n}-1\) is also enormous.  The bound \(4n\) is tiny compared to either.  So the only way the gcd can be large is if \(n\) itself is small.  Let's test with \(n=6\) we got \(9\), which is \(1.5n\).  For \(n=12\) we got \(9\), which is \(0.75n\).  For \(n=18\) we got \(27\), which is \(1.5n\).  So it seems that the gcd can be as large as \(1.5n\) or maybe even \(2n\).  For \(n=6\) we got \(9\) (1.5n).  For \(n=18\) we got \(27\) (1.5n).  For \(n=30\)?  Let's test quickly: \(30^30\) is huge, \(2^{30}-1=1073741823\).  The gcd likely includes \(3^?\) and maybe \(7\)?  But \(30=2\cdot3\cdot5\).  The prime factors of \(2^{30}-1\) include 3, 5, 7, 11, 13, 17, 19, etc.  The common primes are 3 and 5? 5 divides \(2^{30}-1\)? 2^30 вүЎ 0 mod 5? Actually 2^4=16вүЎ1, so 2^30 = (2^4)^7 * 2^2 вүЎ 1^7 * 4 = 4, not 1. So 5 does not divide 2^30-1. 3 does.  So gcd is some power of 3.  The exponent of 3 in 30^30 is 30 * 1 = 30.  The exponent of 3 in 2^30-1 is v_3(2^30-1).  Using LTE for p=3: v_3(2^30-1) = v_3(2-1) + v_3(2+1) + v_3(30) - 1? Actually formula for v_3(2^n-1) with n even: v_3(2^n-1) = v_3(2-1) + v_3(2+1) + v_3(n) - 1? Wait the LTE for p=3 with base numbers 2 and 1? For odd prime p, v_p(a^n - b^n) = v_p(a-b) + v_p(n) if a вүЎ b mod p and p вҲӨ a,b.  Here a=2, b=1. 2-1=1, so v_p(2^n-1) = v_p(2-1) + v_p(n) = v_p(n) for odd primes p that divide 2^n-1? Actually the formula for a^n - b^n when p | a-b and p вҲӨ a,b is v_p(a^n - b^n) = v_p(a-b) + v_p(n).  But here a-b=1, so v_p(a-b)=0.  So v_p(2^n-1) = v_p(n) for odd primes p that divide 2^n-1?  That would give v_3(2^30-1) = v_3(30) = 1.  So 3^1 divides it.  So gcd is at most 3.  That's вүӨ 4*30=120.  So bound holds.

Thus, the gcd bound seems plausible.  However to avoid a messy proof, we can use a different lemma: For any n, any common divisor of n^n and 2^n-1 is a divisor of 4n.  This is a known fact: gcd(n^n, 2^n-1) вүӨ 4n.  The proof can be done by considering the prime factorization and using ZsigmondyвҖҷs theorem to show that if a prime p divides both, then p divides n and also p divides 2^n-1, so the order of 2 modulo p divides n.  Then p вүЎ 1 (mod order).  But that doesnвҖҷt bound p by 4n.  However we can use the fact that if p is an odd prime dividing both n and 2^n-1, then p вүӨ n.  So the product over all such p of p^{v_p(n)} is at most something like (max p)^{something}.  The bound 4n is not obviously a consequence.

For n=18, gcd=27, which is 3^3.  So the gcd is a power of 3, and 3^something.  It seems that the gcd can be as large as something like (3^{v_3(n)})?  Actually for n=6, v_3(6)=1, gcd=9 = 3^2.  For n=18, v_3(18)=2, gcd=27 = 3^3.  For n=54, v_3(54)=3, then 2^54-1 contains 3^?  Using LTE, v_3(2^54-1) = v_3(54) = 3?  Actually formula for p=3 with a=2,b=1: v_3(2^n-1) = v_3(n) for n even? Since v_p(2^n-1) is bounded by something like n * v_p(2^n-1) is not obviously small.  For example, n=6 gives v_3(2^6-1)=2. So gcd includes 3^3.  That's 27.  So the exponent of 3 in gcd is v_3(2^n-1), which can be as large as something like v_3(2^n-1) = v_3(n) + 2?  Actually for p=3, there is a known formula: v_3(2^n-1) = v_3(n) + 1 for n even?  Let's compute n=2: 2^2-1=3, v_3=1, v_3(2)=0, so v_3=1.  n=4: 2^4-1=15, v_3=1, v_3(4)=0, so 1.  n=6: 2^6-1=63, v_3=2, v_3(6)=1, so 2.  n=8: 2^8-1=255, v_3=1, v_3(8)=0, so 1.  n=10: 2^10-1=1023, v_3=1?
